    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10350109618417041095">The first English verse translation of the Georgian epic of adventure and romance written in 12th or 13th century. Urushadze has also produced a popular anthology of Georgian poetry in English. Her preface explains the original metrical structure, which is so complex that sometimes it requires rhyming words to the fourth syllable. She also includes a brief list of Georgian</span>
  <span id="freeText10350109618417041095" style="display:none">The first English verse translation of the Georgian epic of adventure and romance written in 12th or 13th century. Urushadze has also produced a popular anthology of Georgian poetry in English. Her preface explains the original metrical structure, which is so complex that sometimes it requires rhyming words to the fourth syllable. She also includes a brief list of Georgian words she retained, many of them names. An introduction by David M Lang (oriental and African studies, U. of London) puts Rustaveli and his poem in historical context. Annotation c. Book News, Inc., Portland, OR.</span>

<div id="review_1826804973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1826804973" />
    <a title="Syme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7606376-syme"><img alt="Syme" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406199421p2/7606376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1826804973?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Syme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Syme" href="/user/show/7606376-syme">Syme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789941079269" href="/book/show/28472396-the-knight-in-the-panther-s-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1826804973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14325086836473282448">I found this beautifully illustrated book in a nice English book shop in Tbilisi, at the end of my trip through Georgia. As I'd seen the name Rustaveli everywhere (it seems like half the streets in the country are named after the guy) and the title intrigued me, I bought it. I have no regrets.<br/><br/>It's truly an epic poem: 12th century, super strong knights killing whole armies on their own, rescuing princesses, and "brotherly" love (the hero at one point basically leaves his fiancé because he loves t</span>
  <span id="freeText14325086836473282448" style="display:none">I found this beautifully illustrated book in a nice English book shop in Tbilisi, at the end of my trip through Georgia. As I'd seen the name Rustaveli everywhere (it seems like half the streets in the country are named after the guy) and the title intrigued me, I bought it. I have no regrets.<br /><br />It's truly an epic poem: 12th century, super strong knights killing whole armies on their own, rescuing princesses, and "brotherly" love (the hero at one point basically leaves his fiancé because he loves the other hero more, who is oh so beautiful). <br /><br />From what I learnt online, Rustaveli has contributed more to the Georgian identity than say, Shakespeare to the English. This book breathes Georgia, and as I loved the country I can't help loving this as well. So get your ass over there and read this on a nice mountain top or something.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14325086836473282448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1814008057" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1814008057" />
    <a title="Clayton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15306289-clayton"><img alt="Clayton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371623987p2/15306289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1814008057?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clayton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clayton" href="/user/show/15306289-clayton">Clayton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789941079269" href="/book/show/28472396-the-knight-in-the-panther-s-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1814008057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16077761044571144931"><i>I know that in the end you will bless the course that I have taken.<br/>A wise man cannot stand the thought his good friend will be forsaken. <br/>Let me remind you of what Plato said to help us awaken: <br/>'By lies and two-facedness, the body's hurt, the soul is shaken.'</i><br/><br/>This is the national epic of Georgia, admired by readers (and endured by bored students) across the country for centuries. At least according to the people who make these kinds of decisions, Rustaveli is so important that there are Rustaveli </span>
  <span id="freeText16077761044571144931" style="display:none"><i>I know that in the end you will bless the course that I have taken.<br />A wise man cannot stand the thought his good friend will be forsaken. <br />Let me remind you of what Plato said to help us awaken: <br />'By lies and two-facedness, the body's hurt, the soul is shaken.'</i><br /><br />This is the national epic of Georgia, admired by readers (and endured by bored students) across the country for centuries. At least according to the people who make these kinds of decisions, Rustaveli is so important that there are Rustaveli streets and Rustaveli statues across Georgia (I actually bought this at Prospero's Books, a Tblisi bookstore I highly recommend to travelers that you can find on...Rustaveli Avenue!). Hell, this poem is so beloved by Georgians that even the guy who illustrated it is a national hero, and he wasn't even Georgian (Mihaly Zichy, of Hungary). Plus, <i>The Knight in the Panther's Skin</i> is far and away the most badass title for an epic poem I've ever seen, and I've read <i>The Daredevils of Sassoun</i>. <br /><br />The barest of set-ups: Avtandil is an Arab prince in love with the daughter of King Rostevan. One day, while out on a manly hunt with King Rostevan, the men come across a wild man wearing a panther-skin coat who ignores all pleas to engage them in courtesies and handily slaughters dozens of men when challenged before disappearing on his horse. Figuring that the kingdom won't know peace until the murderer is apprehended, young Avtandil sets out to find this dangerous wanderer and prove his worth as a knight and a lover.<br /><br />Against other epic poems, it doesn't stand out for the elegance of its plotting or the originality of its themes, but for my money, Rustaveli's got two things going: narrative rapidity, and philosophical sophistication. This poem <i>moves.</i> Things happen in rapid succession, events pile on events, and stories fold into other stories, with dozens and dozens of chapters in a book just over 350 pages. You see, Avtandil's talking to Tariel about how Tariel met Pridon, and he can't tell the story of meeting Pridon without telling why Pridon was on the run from the assassins, who were hired by Pridon's uncle because...well, read the poem. I love it, personally, and it's hard not to get swept up into the joy of the book, sometimes not so much an epic as a machine to generate stories. <br /><br />That other part, the philosophical complexity, is one of the most fascinating parts of the poem, although one I'm not all that qualified to lecture on. What I do know is that Rustaveli was working in a context where Neoplatonic Christianity was the standard, Greek philosophy was read alongside the Qur'an, the finest poetry was in Persian and the best textiles were from China, and those Franks over in Europa were nothing more than half-literate savages. Suffice it to say, Georgia was at the center of a lot of things in the early 13th century, and those crossroads are felt in the poem. More interesting are the story's development of love and friendship as the highest virtues, so much so that they eclipse more traditional epic values like prowess or loyalty to the state; in fact, patriotism gets a good thwacking at several points as nothing more than a kind of false attachment that keeps the individual from truly developing as a soul dedicated in the service of a friend, a beloved, or God. It's a philosophy of self-fulfillment and joy, where life is best lived as living well in rapturous service to another, fully developed and explored from many angles. <br /><br />A note on translation, because when it comes to 800 year-old poems from non Indo-European languages, translation is everything. I read the 2015 translation by Lynn Coffin, which is distinguished for being the first to preserve the <i>shairi</i> form, which is written in quatrains rhyming a-a-a-a, always with 16 syllables to a line and a caesura (pause) in the middle. What this means is that Coffin is the first writer to preserve both the structure <u>and</u> the meaning of the poem in English. This is an achievement, full stop. But one reason why nobody's tried doing that until so recently is that Rustaveli's form is entirely unnatural to the English ear: English meter is based on stress, not syllables, and English just doesn't rhyme very well compared to most languages, so Coffin's doing two things that the language isn't really meant to do. Inevitably, within the poem's 6,400+ lines, there are some real howlers. The miraculous thing is that there's no more than a few dozen, by my count. Most of it scans just fine, though, and sometimes it even rises to the level of solid English verse that happens to align with medieval Georgian verse, like finding a perfect sonnet in the middle of a physics textbook. The end result keeps a suitably foreign feel, better for chanting and singing, as Rustaveli would no doubt have it, than a sober recital. If you like your big foreign poems big and foreign, I would recommend Coffin's translation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16077761044571144931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_638564367" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/638564367" />
    <a title="Zadignose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose"><img alt="Zadignose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340768521p2/4319285.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/638564367?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zadignose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zadignose" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose">Zadignose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/18055279-the-knight-in-the-panther-s-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319285-zadignose?shelf=12th-century">12th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer638564367" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7959779879056923956">Review so far:<br/><br/>The Book of Infinite Hyperbole, a.k.a. the Book of Crybabies. <br/><br/>No one is <em>halfway</em> affected or <em>slightly</em> emotional. Everyone is in a constant rage of passion or a life-threatening swoon in each and every instant of their lives. Every person is a moon and a sun, and every man is also a lion. All hair is jet, all eyelashes cut, all faces are devastatingly beautiful and more radiant than the sun. Did I mention the sun? It is often mentioned. The book is a constant torrent of tears, so muc</span>
  <span id="freeText7959779879056923956" style="display:none">Review so far:<br /><br />The Book of Infinite Hyperbole, a.k.a. the Book of Crybabies. <br /><br />No one is <em>halfway</em> affected or <em>slightly</em> emotional. Everyone is in a constant rage of passion or a life-threatening swoon in each and every instant of their lives. Every person is a moon and a sun, and every man is also a lion. All hair is jet, all eyelashes cut, all faces are devastatingly beautiful and more radiant than the sun. Did I mention the sun? It is often mentioned. The book is a constant torrent of tears, so much so that I nearly drowned from reading it.<br /><br />The passion is unrelenting in its extremity, and is important in and of itself, regardless of narrative context.<br /><br />There is also occasional surprising sarcasm or irony which catches us off guard and challenges us to decide just how much sincerity we should attribute to the rest of the proclamations of passion, faith, etc. The all-around unreasonableness of the characters also poses a challenge. A king will send his retainer away for three years, virtually on a whim, though he regards the mission as critical, and never seems to miss him, yet will be thrown into a towering rage the next time the same retainer wants to go away for a while, as the king can't bear to part with him. Lovers whose only desire in life is to be together will take it upon themselves to separate for no good reason, and then constantly bewail their separation. Meanwhile, occasional sacred unbreakable oaths will be made then broken for mere convenience because, of course, lovers are too mad to be bound by oaths.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7959779879056923956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2043883995" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2043883995" />

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48327873-markus?shelf=read-in-german">read-in-german</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2043883995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13682777583037317541">Der Recke im Tigerfell (The Knight in Tigerskin) <br/>By Schota Rusthawelli – (about 1172 to 1216)<br/>The 12th-century epic poem, originally composed in the Georgian Language, has in this edition been rewritten in its poetic verse form into the German Language, and in no ordinary way, but in exquisite meter and vocabulary, which in its own right is a masterpiece. <br/>The poem itself is a curious mixture of cultural influences, Persian, Arabic, European knighthood and chivalry.<br/>Rostewan the Arab king, is a her</span>
  <span id="freeText13682777583037317541" style="display:none">Der Recke im Tigerfell (The Knight in Tigerskin) <br />By Schota Rusthawelli – (about 1172 to 1216)<br />The 12th-century epic poem, originally composed in the Georgian Language, has in this edition been rewritten in its poetic verse form into the German Language, and in no ordinary way, but in exquisite meter and vocabulary, which in its own right is a masterpiece. <br />The poem itself is a curious mixture of cultural influences, Persian, Arabic, European knighthood and chivalry.<br />Rostewan the Arab king, is a hero’s name that can also be found in Shahnameh, The Epic of the Persian Kings, by Ferdowsi.<br />The language used in Rusthawelli epic is as colorful and overly romantic as is used in the Persian Shahnameh and in Arabian Nights. <br />But then the Knightly Chivalry motivating its heroes at the service of their faultless virgin maids is well inspired from European Medieval tales like King Arthurs Knights, and so are their outfits of shining armors, metal helmets swords and spear and all. <br />An exceptional book of beauty and romantic poetry.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13682777583037317541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2767185789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13613738353791744226">This is one of the most epically melodramatic stories I have ever read. And I loved every page of it! The nested-stories of Avtandil, Tariel, and Nestan-Darejan are captivating and wonderful. The tears that they shed for each other are ridiculous and lovely. The florid descriptions boggle my mind. It's an excellent adventure of several love-besotted knights whose bromance is the only thing stronger than their wistful love for their distant "suns." <br/><br/>But to be perfectly honest, I kind of expected m</span>
  <span id="freeText13613738353791744226" style="display:none">This is one of the most epically melodramatic stories I have ever read. And I loved every page of it! The nested-stories of Avtandil, Tariel, and Nestan-Darejan are captivating and wonderful. The tears that they shed for each other are ridiculous and lovely. The florid descriptions boggle my mind. It's an excellent adventure of several love-besotted knights whose bromance is the only thing stronger than their wistful love for their distant "suns." <br /><br />But to be perfectly honest, I kind of expected more panther appearances.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2314781640?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamuna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamuna" href="/user/show/54088083-tamuna">Tamuna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789941197536" href="/book/show/17227753-vepkhistqaosani">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2314781640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13067394991148946049">There's a reason exploring and analyzing this poem is its own profession. <br/><br/>I have, at some point, made fun of some parts of it. I've also bashed its raging fanboys and fangirls who shove it into everyone's nose whether it's called for or not and who don't want to admit that it's not for every reader and all tastes. I've criticized people for blind, fanatical sort of devotion to it. I have, therefore, been recognized as a hater who can't appreciate the masterpiece (at this point you may steal a gl</span>
  <span id="freeText13067394991148946049" style="display:none">There's a reason exploring and analyzing this poem is its own profession. <br /><br />I have, at some point, made fun of some parts of it. I've also bashed its raging fanboys and fangirls who shove it into everyone's nose whether it's called for or not and who don't want to admit that it's not for every reader and all tastes. I've criticized people for blind, fanatical sort of devotion to it. I have, therefore, been recognized as a hater who can't appreciate the masterpiece (at this point you may steal a glance at my five stars rating and become quite confused, you may want to keep reading). Because of this, I deem myself as a pretty unbiased source of valuable feedback for those who don't know what they're facing, so please, bear with me for a bit.<br /><br />Whom is this poem NOT for? - If you're looking for believable characters with complex personalities that feel like real people, intricate plot that develops them to their fullest potential, provide good role models and blow your mind with the story arc and so on... this is the wrong story for you to pick. <br />But the thing is, the above mentioned were not the objective of this work and its virtues lie elsewhere. Let me list them:<br /><br />1. You won't even know what you read. Written in the twelfth century, this poem was a valuable piece of one of a kind political and social commentary on (back then) present day Georgia and its state, its politics, worldview and philosophy, giving both criticism and appreciation, while pretending to be a somewhat linear story. The characters presented to the reader are not really people at all and shouldn't be treated as such by a reader. Likewise the countries where it all takes place are not the countries they claim to be. A single character can be an allegory of one political figure, one side and role of such a figure, and a whole country - all at once, and multiple characters can be combined into a single important figure of that time. The same is true for locations, so don't expect accurate representations of Arabia or India. In truth the Arabia and India have no role in the poem. The author uses intricate wordplay and double (triple, quadruple) meanings in describing events, characters and names, cleverly smuggling the precious information and hiding it in plain sight. There are stanzas we had to learn by heart at school and only years after graduating did I discover hidden (and sometimes rather bold and even naughty) meanings in some of them. I had recited those lines over and over again and never had realized ALL the things I was saying.<br />Needless to say, no translation in the world can ever do it justice. All that covertly shared information is lost the moment a single word changes. Even adapting it from old Georgian to modern Georgian will cause a devastating loss. Being at least somewhat proficient in old Georgian is essential, or most of it will go over the reader's head. It actually happens to most of us - Georgians - when we first get acquainted with it. On the other hand, it's always an adventure to take the book and read it through a nth time, hunting for new discoveries.<br /><br />2. The language. Aside from wordplay, there's a lot to say about the richness of the language used. Georgian by its nature is more of an expressive language than an analytical one and the poet has taken full advantage of the fact, using exquisite enchanting vocabulary while preserving flawless rhyme throughout the entire giant of a poem. Again, translation cannot do it justice.<br /><br />3. Informational. There are many things about the cultural side of historical Georgia we wouldn't have known without The Knight in Panther's Skin, or wouldn't have known as well as we do now. I'm speaking about ancient traditions and social norms, leftovers of pagan beliefs, dozens of now extinct local musical instruments, old astronomical names and many, many other things. It's a treasure trove of recovering the cultural history.<br /><br />4. Just the fact that it embraces Renaissance before there was Renaissance is a valuable trait in and of itself. The poem was frowned upon by the church, to put it mildly. While being very much in tune with spirituality, godliness and the notion of greater good, the poem ignored the subject of religion per se. No specific religion is ever mentioned and the characters can either be seen praying to a nameless one true sort of God, or pleading celestial bodies. Instead it focuses on the ideals of knighthood and humanism, bringing them as the common ground for the characters to build their relationship on.<br /><br />All in all, five stars are not even enough. It is true that it's hard to understand and I will always argue that when we studied it at school, we probably weren't ready to take it in, except for a precious few individuals, maybe. It also wasn't taught from the aspect it should've been, giving praise where there was less praise needed while completely ignoring its true value. But if any of the above listed items speak to you, it's not a poem to be missed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13067394991148946049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8980273231816302211" style="display:none">How could I give this epic poem, pride of the land of Georgia, any less than 5 stars?   <span id="freeText15360187256084299142" style="display:none">I don't really have the capacity to give this any sort of starred rating, but I will say, it was a deeply interesting read. These things are always better appreciated in their language of origin, and, suffice it to say, I do not read Georgian. Moreover, I am entirely unfamiliar with the Persian epic tradition on which Rustaveli is drawing. The most resonant elements for me were the depictions of courtly, knightly affection, that bear some resemblance to the relationships described in some of the medieval French poetry I've read; one wonders about the extent of cultural contact informing artistic exchange here, given Georgia's geographical situation as the crossroads of European and Middle Eastern empires. </span>

  <span id="freeText9349857153226738649" style="display:none">(Note: I just realized this is not the edition I read. It aims to be a double love story, but it ends up being a tale of an epic friendship that doesn't only help love win in the end, but also makes three kingdoms find peace and unity. Great story, part of the world's epic heritage, definitely a must-read for storytellers.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14391864286852391888">Ms. Coffin has performed an amazing feat here in this translation of an epic poem from 12th century Georgia to verse that maintains the poetic meter and beauty of the original. I brought this book (ISBN 978-9941-0-7926-9) with me in my Christmas visit to family, and, as it seems to me that poetry is best when read aloud or heard, we took turns reading chapters to each other. It was a very memorable Christmas.</span>

          </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review92651880">
  <td colspan="2">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hayk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hayk" href="/user/show/115189080-hayk">Hayk</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2242018007725565617">First thing first this is not the national epic of the Georgian people. Actually Rustaveli took the plot from a Persian story and rendered it in Georgian. <br/><br/>I’ve listened to the original Georgian audio version of this book and it was fascinating. Maybe because of the valuable influence that the poem has had on Georgian language, “The Knight In the Panthers skin” is a must-read if you are Georgian. But the story itself wasn’t really the best medieval romance or poem I have ever read. I mean no offe</span>
  <span id="freeText2242018007725565617" style="display:none">First thing first this is not the national epic of the Georgian people. Actually Rustaveli took the plot from a Persian story and rendered it in Georgian. <br /><br />I’ve listened to the original Georgian audio version of this book and it was fascinating. Maybe because of the valuable influence that the poem has had on Georgian language, “The Knight In the Panthers skin” is a must-read if you are Georgian. But the story itself wasn’t really the best medieval romance or poem I have ever read. I mean no offense, I adore the Georgian culture and the language, but I wish someone could slap the heroes of this book and tell them to not whelp and stop being drama queens. <br /><br />What I mean is the plot was simple and the only peculiarity of the main characters (apart from being valiant and brave when it came to battles but extremely overdramatic when being in love) was that they were super emotional and they could weep, cry tears of blood and pass out in every single chapter of the book. I haven’t counted how many times the word “weep” was used in the story. And it seems that Rustaveli wasn’t really prudent in describing beauty as the only words used for hyperbola or metaphors were the words sun, jet, roses and aloe tree. The continuation of these words was really too much. By the way Rustaveli was kind of teasing the so-called poets in the prologue who weren’t proficient in describing things or to rhyme words and write a decent story. Maybe he was indeed a great poet Georgian but the story was boring and an utterly overenthusiastic piece of crap.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2242018007725565617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7319689361795332496">Romances in the chivalric tradition, involving knights on a quest, heroic battles and beautiful women, were common in mediaeval times. This one was written by a Georgian courtier (possibly based on a now lost Persian folk tale) and is regarded as the national epic.<br/>It was written in verse, but I read a prose translation, so cannot comment on the poetry. (The edition is not available on Goodreads.) As a story it is better than most chivalric romances; the characters, including the female ones, are</span>
  <span id="freeText7319689361795332496" style="display:none">Romances in the chivalric tradition, involving knights on a quest, heroic battles and beautiful women, were common in mediaeval times. This one was written by a Georgian courtier (possibly based on a now lost Persian folk tale) and is regarded as the national epic.<br />It was written in verse, but I read a prose translation, so cannot comment on the poetry. (The edition is not available on Goodreads.) As a story it is better than most chivalric romances; the characters, including the female ones, are more like people instead of abstract embodiments of some courtly ideal. Some of the imagery and drama probably comes from an Eastern tradition (and sometimes seems hyperbolic in prose form). I have to give it five stars for being an excellent example of its genre.</span>

  <span id="freeText14561146014811309244" style="display:none">The height of hyperbole, the extremes of emotion and the salience of the sun<br /><br />Written around 1200, this is reportedly one of the most appreciated Georgian works, so much so that once upon a time, brides received a copy of this upon moving to a new home. Then the Church happened and a copy of it was recovered squirreled away behind the appendix of a theological work and it was finally published in 1712. <br /><br />The work is a striking hybrid of West and East. On one hand, the chivalry of Avt'handil and Tariel and others call to mind arthurian tales and the French chansons de geste. On the other, the aesthetic, the descriptions, the story-within-a-story structure, they bring to mind 1001 nights</span>

<div id="review_3101012482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3101012482" />
    <a title="Øystein Brekke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37034288-ystein-brekke"><img alt="Øystein Brekke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571388323p2/37034288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3101012482?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Øystein Brekke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Øystein Brekke" href="/user/show/37034288-ystein-brekke">Øystein Brekke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/20571903-the-knight-in-the-panther-s-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3101012482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9117706083844810543">This was an enjoyable read! Reading 13th century epic poetry is sometimes more for the curiosity factor or the historical value, and not so much for entertainment - and by all means, there is a lot of that here, but I was also actually entertained!<br/><br/>The Knight in the Panther's Skin is, I suppose, something of a national epic of Georgia, written in verse in the 13th century. But it doesn't actually take place in Georgia, in fact I don't think the name 'Georgia' appears anywhere in it - it is set ma</span>
  <span id="freeText9117706083844810543" style="display:none">This was an enjoyable read! Reading 13th century epic poetry is sometimes more for the curiosity factor or the historical value, and not so much for entertainment - and by all means, there is a lot of that here, but I was also actually entertained!<br /><br />The Knight in the Panther's Skin is, I suppose, something of a national epic of Georgia, written in verse in the 13th century. But it doesn't actually take place in Georgia, in fact I don't think the name 'Georgia' appears anywhere in it - it is set mainly in Arabia and India. And it isn't primarily a tale of epic battles. This is a love story. An extremely over-the-top love story, but still. The protagonist, Avtandil, is sent to search for the Knight in the Panther's Skin, a mysterious weeping warrior that no one knows who is. The knight - who turns out to be called Tariel (one wonders if Tolkien has found some inspiration for elvish names here) - is wandering the wilderness full of despair because his love, Nestan-Darejan has been kidnapped be evil people?beings? and he believes her lost forever. Tariel is the perfect warrior, and his love is the perfect maiden - we are talking so perfect that people go blind from looking at them because they are so radiant. <br /><br />Themes of love - between Tariel and Avtandil and their respective love interests - and of friendship and loyalty - between Tariel and Avtandil - are developed. And of course evil loses in the end, as the narrator assures us that it always will.<br /><br />It is a challenging read, yes - a 13th century epic poem would be - but I found it worth it, and it was very interesting to have a glimpse of a time and culture that I know very little about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9117706083844810543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2904060091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2904060091" />
    <a title="Sally" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5733902-sally"><img alt="Sally" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591019856p2/5733902.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2904060091?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sally" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sally" href="/user/show/5733902-sally">Sally</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/20571903-the-knight-in-the-panther-s-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5733902-sally?shelf=12th-century-literature">12th-century-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5733902-sally?shelf=georgian-literature">georgian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2904060091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10051492395733295470">This 12th century work is considered the Georgian epic. Written in poewtry, this version is rendered into prose by translator Katharinew Vivian, which I think is essential for the modern reader.<br/>In 200 pages, we read a tale that makes one think of the Arabian Nights, medieval works of courtly love, fairy tales and the Odyssey. Certainly its location meant that the Georgiasns had much contact with Pesians, Arabs, Greeks and others.<br/>As a 21st century reader, I felt it was long enough. Although it's </span>
  <span id="freeText10051492395733295470" style="display:none">This 12th century work is considered the Georgian epic. Written in poewtry, this version is rendered into prose by translator Katharinew Vivian, which I think is essential for the modern reader.<br />In 200 pages, we read a tale that makes one think of the Arabian Nights, medieval works of courtly love, fairy tales and the Odyssey. Certainly its location meant that the Georgiasns had much contact with Pesians, Arabs, Greeks and others.<br />As a 21st century reader, I felt it was long enough. Although it's perfectly readable- lots of peerlessly beautiful princesses, all-action heroes, wise but fearsome kings, boundless treasure.... Although purportedly set in distant lands (Arabia, India, China, the 'land of the Seas') , the characters bear Georgian names (one addresses another in Georgian at one point) so I think we can take it that it's based on the author's homeland.<br />Along with the adventures, are occasional asides based on Christian precepts, while knight Avtandil's lecture to the melancholy and despondent Tariel feel like a 12th century attempt to deal with depression.<br />I read this to get a background to Georgian writing. It's OK- and may be superb if you can read the original poetry - but certainly a look at a distant culture.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10051492395733295470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2026740813" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2026740813" />
    <a title="Rex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32635507-rex"><img alt="Rex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465019765p2/32635507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2026740813?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rex" href="/user/show/32635507-rex">Rex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/34762182-the-knight-in-the-panther-skin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=platonism">platonism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=epics-and-folk-literature">epics-and-folk-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=caucasus">caucasus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=to-reread">to-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32635507-rex?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2026740813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12925250659586874050">This is a beautiful edition of a great epic. Coffin's translation tries to imitate the Georgian poetic form; some of the rhymes feel more than a little forced, but it ceases to be distracting as one grows accustomed to it, and that consistent rhythm does evoke the stylistic beauty of the original. The afterward by Levan Gigineishvili is also well worth the read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2688447431" />
    <a title="Dato Abuashvili" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92663841-dato-abuashvili"><img alt="Dato Abuashvili" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2688447431?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dato Abuashvili" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dato Abuashvili" href="/user/show/92663841-dato-abuashvili">Dato Abuashvili</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2688447431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14813523778582653053">This is not just a story of great warriors and also great lovers. Whole story is built on life's main values like friendship, bravery, love, justice, honor, etc... Dive deep into the breathtaking story of two knights who will become best friends while helping each other on an epic journey. Medieval warrior's life can not be described better than this! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_9754154'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9754154"><img alt="დიდოსტატის კონსტანტინეს მარჯვენა" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1386421153l/9754154._SX98_.jpg" />

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('a54hmfKrLIJzS3cheEI5y+IIU8qgfIew7Iqini8afdqzFedcUVoAWopt7Jo/m4y+wo1R295z0RfSyDuPOEo2vg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F+m0Gl2K1biQLPmU1L/ufxO3HpounQcskjzaWrMSSaTPYnLf/nv5YGkKYi+TZlsKMzIci1CSUYusfkNLpEICwA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5475135.Shota_Rustaveli">About Shota Rustaveli</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5475135.Shota_Rustaveli"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1350473992p3/5475135.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5475135.Shota_Rustaveli">Shota Rustaveli
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9255930758895457399">Shota Rustaveli (Georgian: შოთა რუსთაველი) (born approx. c. 1160 – died after c. 1220), was a Georgian poet of the 12th century, and one of the greatest contributors to Georgian literature. He is author of "The Knight in the Panther's Skin" (ვეფხისტყაოსანი, Vepkhistkaosani), the Georgian national epic poem.<br/>Little, if anything, is known about Rustaveli from contemporary sources. His poem itself, na</span>
  <span id="freeText9255930758895457399" style="display:none">Shota Rustaveli (Georgian: შოთა რუსთაველი) (born approx. c. 1160 – died after c. 1220), was a Georgian poet of the 12th century, and one of the greatest contributors to Georgian literature. He is author of "The Knight in the Panther's Skin" (ვეფხისტყაოსანი, Vepkhistkaosani), the Georgian national epic poem.<br />Little, if anything, is known about Rustaveli from contemporary sources. His poem itself, namely the prologue, provides a clue to his identity: the poet identifies himself as "a certain Rustveli." "Rustveli" is not a surname, but a territorial epithet which can be interpreted as "of/from/holder of Rustavi." Later Georgian authors of the 15th–18th centuries are more informative: they are almost unanimous in identifying him as Shota Rustaveli, a name which is preserved on a fresco and a document from the formerly Georgian Monastery of the Holy Cross at Jerusalem. The fresco was described by the Georgian pilgrim Timote Gabashvili in 1757/58, and rediscovered by a team of Georgian scholars in 1960. The same Jerusalem document speaks of Shota as a sponsor of the monastery and a "high treasurer," thus echoing a popular legend that Rustaveli was a minister at Queen Tamar’s court and retired to the monastery at an advanced age. Both a folk tradition and the 17th century royal poet Archil identify Rustaveli as a native of the southern Georgian region of Meskheti, where his home village Rustavi was located (not to be confused with the modern-day city of Rustavi near Tbilisi). He is assumed to have been born between 1160 and 1165. A legend states that Rustaveli was educated at the medieval Georgian academies of Gelati and Ikalto, and then in "Greece" (i.e., the Byzantine Empire). He must have produced his major work no earlier than the 1180s and no later than the first decade of the 13th century, most probably c. 1205–1207.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9255930758895457399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
